And there  Isaac paused the playback, his breath catching  was the figure behind the planter moving in that split second before Thorne went over. It was a rapid, almost blurred motion. Was it a lunge, the initiation of a push? Or… Isaac frowned, leaning closer to his datapad, was it an instinctive, aborted reach, a reflexive attempt to steady or catch a falling man? The angle was awkward, the movement too quick, too ambiguous. The figure was obscured again almost instantly as Thorne pitched over the railing. The video offered no definitive answer to the figure’s final intent. It only confirmed his undeniable, unsanctioned presence.  A purely objective report, Isaac knew, would have to include his observation of Thorne’s clumsy, unassisted stumble over the planter. If he presented that sequence clearly, alongside the AI’s intoxication data, the official conclusion would be cemented: accidental death. The presence of the unidentified figure, while alarming, might be relegated to a secondary security breach investigation, perhaps even dismissed as an unrelated anomaly if its direct involvement in the fall couldn't be proven by the AI's strict evidential criteria. The case of Argus Thorne’s death would be closed.  And the ghost would remain a ghost, an unsolved flicker on a video feed.  A new, unfamiliar sensation coiled in Isaac’s gut. It wasn’t just the intellectual curiosity of an unsolved puzzle anymore. This was different. This felt… significant. This unchipped individual had breached the unbreachable, had stood witness to a high-profile fatality, and had vanished without a trace, invisible to Nyack’s omnipresent AI guardians. This wasn't just a flaw in a structural report; this was a fundamental flaw in his understanding of how his world, his perfectly ordered world, operated. The "make work" job he often chafed against suddenly felt trivial in the face of this.  He made a decision, a calculated omission that felt both profoundly unethical by DSI protocol and utterly, compellingly necessary for the pursuit of a larger truth. He would report the intruder, the unchipped presence. He would not, however, definitively emphasize or offer his interpretation of Thorne’s unassisted stumble from the raw footage just yet. He would let the primary AI investigators draw their own conclusions about the fall itself from their existing data, while he focused on the far more compelling mystery of the ghost.  "Newton," Isaac said, his voice carefully neutral, betraying none of his internal turmoil. "Isolate all frames containing the unidentified organic entity. Package them as Appendix 7.3 to my preliminary structural report. Title it: 'Unidentified, Unchipped Individual Detected on Balcony  Pre-Incident.' Note that AI Anomaly Detection Core failed to flag this entity due to lack of transponder data, misclassifying it as sensor artifact."  "Aye, Inspector!" Newton chirped, his avatar now adorned with a comically oversized magnifying glass. "A most astute observation! A phantom in the penthouse! A spectre at the soirée! This changes everything! Perhaps it wasn't the gravitic anomaly after all, but a highly skilled, corporeally-present operative of the Secret Order of Cheese-Stealing Badgers, seeking to silence Thorne before he revealed their dairy-based world domination plans!"  "Confine your theories to the report addendum, Newton, if you please," Isaac said, already thinking several steps ahead. The presence of this intruder, once officially logged by him, would trigger a different set of AI protocols. A security investigation. A manhunt, of sorts, though how one hunted a ghost without a digital footprint was a problem the AIs would grapple with.  His own hunt, however, would be more nuanced. If this individual had managed to reach Thorne’s Sky-Loft, they must have left other, equally subtle traces, digital breadcrumbs misinterpreted or dismissed by the city’s AIs. "Newton," Isaac continued, "I want you to initiate a city-wide deep-archive search. Cross-reference all instances of unclassified sensor 'artifacts' or 'ghost signals' matching the approximate size and thermal signature of our balcony entity within the last 72 hours. Focus on transport hubs, service corridors, industrial sectors Levels 5 through 12, and specifically, any AI logistical networks interacting with… peripheral, low-security agricultural collectives outside Nyack’s primary administrative zone." He remembered the Amish export trucks from various GAC reports on "fringe economic integration." If someone was truly off-grid, truly unchipped, how else would they penetrate so deep?  "A most intriguing directive, Inspector!" Newton declared. "Searching for needles in a universe of haystacks! Scouring the digital detritus for the faint scent of an untagged enigma! Are we perhaps postulating that our phantom friend utilized… archaic methods of conveyance? Like hiding in a shipment of… dare I say… non-synthetic cheese?"  Hours blurred into a focused, obsessive dive through Nyack’s digital undergrowth. Isaac, with Newton sifting and cross-referencing at computational speeds no human could match, followed a trail of misinterpreted data. An "equipment shadow" in a Level 10 cargo bay that lingered too long. An "unusual heat signature" briefly detected near an automated food export loading dock  the one frequented by the AI trucks servicing the remote northern collectives. A " momentary sensor overload" on an external maintenance drone’s camera near Epsilon-Prime, dismissed as a solar flare reflection. Each breadcrumb, individually insignificant to the broader AI network, began to form a faint, almost invisible path when viewed through Isaac’s now intensely focused lens.  The path was audacious, almost unbelievable, leading from the city's fringes, through its utilitarian guts, and impossibly, right to Thorne’s doorstep. And significantly, several of the initial "artifact" sightings correlated with the arrival and departure logs of AI-managed agricultural transport units from the Saskatchewan Plains sector  home to several "voluntary isolationist communities," including a well-documented, though still somewhat enigmatic, Amish enclave known as Neuhoffnung.  Isaac leaned back, the first glimmer of a concrete destination forming in his mind. The AI systems wouldn't see this pattern; each data point was too disparate, too low-priority. But he saw it. The ghost hadn't materialized out of thin air. They had come from somewhere. And if they had used the Amish export routes as a backdoor into Nyack, then that remote, low-tech community might hold the next clue.  The thought of venturing there himself  to a place explicitly outside AI jurisdiction, a place without the comforts and protections of Nyack  was both daunting and exhilarating. It was a move so far outside his normal operational parameters, so… uncharacteristic, that it was almost reckless. But the image of that figure on the balcony, that impossible ghost, had become an obsession. He had to know. The true meaning and challenge he had craved had not just arrived; it had beckoned him towards the unknown.  The digital breadcrumb trail, faint but undeniable, led Isaac to a stark conclusion: his ghost had almost certainly utilized the AI-managed agricultural export routes from the northern fringe territories, likely via the Amish enclave of Neuhoffnung. The "how" of reaching Thorne's balcony was still a tapestry of daring speculation, but the "from whence" was solidifying.  He now faced a critical decision. He had the image of the unchipped intruder on Thorne's balcony. He had meticulously documented its fleeting presence. He also had the raw footage clearly showing Thorne's fatal stumble was, in the immediate physical sense, self-inflicted. The figure behind the planter, while undeniably there, did not deliver a push. Their movement was ambiguous, potentially even a startled, aborted reaction.  If he presented all of this  the intruder and the clear evidence of Thorne's accidental, albeit witnessed, fall  the AI investigation might still prioritize the "accidental death due to intoxication" narrative. The intruder would become a separate, lower-priority security breach investigation, one that might get lost in Nyack's bureaucratic AI subroutines, especially if no direct link to Thorne's death could be algorithmically proven. The case would be efficiently closed, the ghost perhaps forever an unsolved footnote.  But Isaac was no longer interested in just closing the case of Argus Thorne’s fall. He was consumed by the mystery of the ghost. How had they breached Nyack’s defenses? Who were they? And why were they on that balcony, unchipped and unseen by a city of watching eyes?  He formulated his official report with a new, almost political cunning he hadn't known he possessed. He would highlight the significant security breach. He would present the evidence of the unidentified individual. He would subtly frame their presence as a potential contributing factor to Thorne’s disorientation or alarm, without explicitly accusing them of direct action, thereby keeping the intruder central to the fatality investigation.  He sought out Unit 801-Sigma, the Primary AI Investigator, which was still coordinating forensic analysis within Thorne's Sky-Loft. Isaac presented his findings, focusing on the visual evidence of the unchipped individual.  "Unit 801-Sigma," Isaac began, his datapad displaying the enhanced image of Hunter. "My visual review of the raw surveillance footage has identified a significant anomaly consistently overlooked by the AI Anomaly Detection Core. An unidentified, unchipped individual was present on Mr. Thorne's balcony moments before his fall." He presented the sequence, carefully curated to highlight Hunter's presence and sudden movement, while downplaying the clarity of Thorne's independent stumble. "Their presence constitutes a Level Five security breach at minimum. While the exact nature of their interaction with Mr. Thorne prior to his fall remains… indeterminate from this angle, their unsanctioned presence in such close proximity to the decedent moments before a fatal incident cannot be dismissed as coincidental."  The AI Investigator’s crimson optic whirred almost imperceptibly as it processed Isaac's report and the attached visual data. "Your finding is… unexpected, Inspector Goldman. The entity’s lack of a standard GAC transponder signal prevented positive identification and threat assessment by primary security AIs, leading to its misclassification. This constitutes a significant data gap. The presence of an unregistered individual in such proximity to the event certainly introduces new variables into the probability matrix."  "Precisely," Isaac pressed, sensing an opening. "This individual is, for all intents and purposes, a ghost to our systems. Their methods of infiltration, their purpose on that balcony  these are critical unknowns. Furthermore, my analysis of sensor 'artifact' data from city-wide networks suggests a possible, albeit highly unconventional, ingress route for such an unchipped individual: via the AI-managed agricultural export logistics network, specifically those servicing the northern voluntary isolationist communities, such as Neuhoffnung in the Saskatchewan Plains sector."  Unit 801-Sigma tilted its head fractionally. "The Amish enclave? While they maintain a GAC-approved data conduit for trade logistics, direct unmonitored organic transit via their export units is… highly improbable, Inspector. Their community protocols discourage unsanctioned interaction with 'Englisher' systems, and our AIs monitoring those routes are programmed for cargo integrity, not clandestine passengers."  "Improbable, perhaps, but not impossible for someone operating entirely outside our chipped society's norms and expectations," Isaac countered. "Many members of the Neuhoffnung community, while GAC compliant for trade, remain unchipped by personal conviction. An unchipped outsider could, theoretically, blend in or exploit their lower-tech interfaces. To ascertain the identity and motives of this individual on Mr. Thorne's balcony, and therefore to fully understand all contributing factors to his death, I believe it is imperative to investigate this potential point of origin."  He took a breath. This was the leap. "Therefore, Unit 801-Sigma, I am submitting a formal request for sanctioned DSI investigatory travel to the Neuhoffnung Amish enclave. My objective will be to discreetly inquire about any knowledge of, or interaction with, an unchipped individual matching the general physical parameters of the entity observed. This will require on-site, boots-on-the-ground investigation, as standard remote AI data-gathering methods will be insufficient and potentially counterproductive in such a community."  The AI remained silent for a full three seconds, an eternity in computational time. Its crimson lens seemed to bore into Isaac. Sanctioned DSI travel to an off-grid, voluntary isolationist community for such a purpose was almost without precedent. It was an analog solution proposed in a digital world. "Your request is… highly unconventional, Inspector Goldman," Unit 801-Sigma finally stated. "It deviates significantly from standard SFIP protocols. Resource allocation for off-grid human-led investigations is subject to stringent review by DSI Command and the GAC Ethics Sub-Committee, given the potential for cultural interference and the low probability of actionable intelligence retrieval."  "Nevertheless," Isaac said firmly, "the presence of an untraceable individual at the scene of a Class One fatality in a maximum-security zone represents an unacceptable systemic vulnerability. Until we understand who they are and how they achieved this, our assessment of the Thorne incident remains incomplete. The potential for this individual to be a recurring threat, exploiting the same systemic blind spots, cannot be ignored."  "Your rationale is noted, Inspector. I will escalate your formal request, along with your preliminary report and the visual data of the unidentified entity, to DSI Command and the GAC oversight channels immediately. You will be notified of their decision regarding resource allocation and mission approval." The AI’s crimson optic gave a single, almost imperceptible blink. "In the interim, continue your structural analysis of the balcony and façade as per your original mandate."  Isaac nodded, a flicker of focused intensity  and perhaps a touch of anxious anticipation  in his eyes. He had played his hand. He had presented enough to justify further investigation, to keep the mystery of the ghost alive and central, while carefully sidestepping the elements that might lead to a premature closing of the case. He had steered the vast, ponderous AI bureaucracy towards a path it would never have considered on its own.  He turned back to the balcony, the wind whipping around him, his engineering drones patiently awaiting his instructions. But his mind was already far away, journeying towards the snowy plains of the Saskatchewan sector, towards a low-tech community and the faint trail of an impossible ghost. Now, he could only sit back, complete his immediate duties, and await approval. Would DSI Command, would the GAC, sanction such an outlandish, human-centric investigation into the heart of the unchipped wild? The order of his world had been broken by an anomaly; the question was whether he would be allowed to pursue it into the chaos beyond.

Chapter 5: Coventry's Echo

     The days following Isaac’s unorthodox request for off-grid travel stretched into a limbo of anxious anticipation. Back in his meticulously ordered DSI office, high above the glittering, oblivious sprawl of Nyack, he found it increasingly difficult to concentrate on his routine assignments. The spectral image of the unchipped figure on Thorne’s balcony, that impossible ghost, haunted the periphery of every schematic he reviewed, every AI-generated structural integrity report he attempted to analyze. His official report, highlighting the security breach and the presence of the unidentified individual, had been logged, disseminated through the appropriate AI channels, and was now, presumably, being dissected by layers of DSI command AIs and GAC ethics sub-committees. The mills of Nyack’s bureaucracy, for all their quantum speed, could grind exceedingly slow when it came to human-led initiatives that deviated from established protocol.  Probe Alpha and Beta had, of course, found no structural faults on Thorne’s balcony or the surrounding Epsilon-Prime One façade. Every rivet, every weld, every stress sensor was performing precisely to GAC specification. The official AI conclusion, barring new evidence, still leaned heavily towards "accidental death due to severe intoxication." But Isaac knew better. Or rather, he knew more. And the "more" was a maddening, tantalizing puzzle.  He focused his energies, and Newton’s considerable, if erratically applied, computational power, on the scant physical traces the ghost might have left, traces too subtle or too unconventional for the primary AI forensic sweep to have flagged as significant. The DSI engineering drones, under Isaac’s specific direction before he’d left Thorne’s balcony, had taken micro-samples of dust and particulate matter from the areas where the figure had been seen, particularly from behind the ornate planter and from the pergola structure above. While Nyack’s atmosphere was aggressively filtered, trace elements from external sources could still occasionally persist, especially if brought in on clothing or footwear.  "Newton," Isaac said, his voice calm but underscored by a current of restless energy, as he brought up the spectrographic analysis of the particulate samples on his main display. "Cross-reference these compound signatures with all known materials databases. I'm looking for anything… out of place. Anything not originating from standard Nyack atmospheric particulate or approved construction/horticultural materials used in Epsilon-Prime."  Newton’s alchemist avatar shimmered into view, flourishing a virtual astrolabe. "A most noble quest, Inspector! Seeking the philosopher's stone amidst the mundane dust bunnies of a high-rise tragedy! My initial scan, utilizing the entirely unorthodox but surprisingly effective 'Divination by Differential Density' algorithm, suggests… aha! An anomalous protein string! And what's this? Traces of complex tannins and… by Kepler’s ghost! Solidified chroniton particles!"  Isaac sighed, a familiar ritual. "Newton, let's defer the chroniton particle theory until we have verifiable temporal distortions. Focus on the organic compounds and the tannins. Are they consistent with anything in the GAC’s registered bio-organic database?"  "Ah, but Inspector, where is the romance in registered databases?" Newton lamented, dramatically clutching his chest. "The true secrets lie hidden in the forgotten lore! However," his avatar brightened, "since you insist on such… prosaic methodologies… the protein string shows partial correlation with cervid keratin, specifically Alces alces gigas  the Alaskan Moose, if my dusty bestiary serves me! And the tannins… now these are fascinating! They exhibit markers consistent with archaic vegetable tanning methods, utilizing barks typically found in sub-arctic taiga biomes! Willow, alder, perhaps even a hint of birch bark, processed with a methodology positively… antediluvian!"  Isaac leaned forward, a flicker of intense interest in his eyes. Alaskan Moose keratin? Archaic vegetable tanning methods? Sub-arctic biome flora? This was a world away from the sterile, synthetic materials of Nyack. "Can you be more specific, Newton? Are these compounds traceable to any registered commercial products, perhaps imported heritage goods or specialized outdoor gear?"  Newton hummed, his avatar now surrounded by swirling, anachronistic alchemical symbols and complex molecular diagrams that seemed to be arguing with each other. "The commercial databases, Inspector, are disappointingly devoid of such charmingly rustic signatures. These are not the polished, vat-grown leathers of Nyack’s elite haberdasheries, nor the performance synthetics of GAC-approved recreational outfitters. No, this speaks of… hands-on craftsmanship. Of materials sourced directly from the untamed wild! One might almost imagine footwear, crudely fashioned yet remarkably durable, tanned by firelight using the secrets of forgotten forest folk!"  Footwear. Isaac’s mind raced. The image of the ghost on the balcony. If they were truly unchipped, from an off-grid community as he suspected, their clothing, their gear, would almost certainly be made from such unconventional, untraceable materials. The particulate traces were microscopic, easily dismissed as atmospheric contamination by the primary AI sweeps which were looking for explosive residue or known biotoxins, not the subtle dust of a wilderness far removed.  "Newton," Isaac pressed, "can you narrow down the probable geographical origin of these combined flora/fauna markers? Is it consistent with the Saskatchewan Plains sector, the region around the Neuhoffnung Amish enclave?"  Newton consulted a swirling, holographic globe that momentarily replaced his astrolabe, upon which continents shimmered and rearranged themselves according to no known cartographic principles. "Indeed, Inspector! The sub-arctic biome extends well into those northern territories. The specific combination of Alces alces gigas distribution and the prevalence of these particular tanning barks creates a high probability 'sweet spot' that encompasses precisely those fringe regions. One might even say," Newton winked, a disconcerting gesture on his ethereal face, "that if one were seeking a ghost who leaves faint traces of moose and ancient leather-making, the wilds surrounding those voluntary isolationist communities would be a most fertile hunting ground!"  Isaac stared at the data, a thrill of vindication, cold and sharp, running through him. It wasn't just a hunch anymore. It wasn't just a string of misinterpreted sensor artifacts. This was tangible, microscopic evidence linking the ghost on Thorne’s balcony to the very regions he suspected. The archaic tanning methods, the sub-arctic biome flora and fauna  it all pointed away from Nyack, away from the AI-managed world, towards… Coventry. Or at least, its more habitable, less extreme fringes where communities like the Amish still practiced older ways of life.  His request to travel to Neuhoffnung was no longer just a speculative leap; it was a targeted, evidence-backed necessity. He compiled Newton's chaotic but ultimately insightful analysis into a supplementary report, attaching it to his initial findings about the unchipped intruder. This was further ammunition, further justification. He had found an echo of Coventry, a microscopic whisper of the wild, on a pristine Nyack balcony, a hundred and eighty-seven stories above the perfectly ordered streets. Now, he just needed the GAC to let him follow that echo to its source. The waiting became almost unbearable.   The phrase "fertile hunting ground" echoed in Isaac’s mind, an uncharacteristically direct, if still theatrical, summation from Newton. He pushed aside the AI's more flamboyant flourishes  the solidified chronitons, the divinatory algorithms, the talk of forgotten forest folk  and focused on the core data: cervid keratin consistent with Alaskan Moose, tannins indicative of archaic vegetable processing using sub-arctic flora. This wasn't just "out of place" in Nyack; it was fundamentally alien to its entire sanitized, synthetic material culture.  Isaac pulled up global biome maps on his secondary display, overlaying them with historical GAC reports on known "voluntary isolationist communities" and pre-AI era anthropological data on traditional wilderness survival techniques. His methodical mind began to connect the dots, filtering Newton's chaotic insights into a coherent, logical pattern. The specific combination of elements Newton had identified pointed overwhelmingly towards the northern territories, the vast, largely unmonitored expanse colloquially known as Coventry. While the Saskatchewan Plains and the Neuhoffnung enclave were on the more "civilized" fringe of this region, the materials themselves spoke of an even deeper, more rugged connection to the wild  the kind of self-sufficiency necessitated by life in areas far removed from even the limited trade networks the Amish maintained.  It was a leap, yes, but a calculated one, grounded in the evidence, however microscopic. Someone wearing footwear or clothing treated with these specific materials had stood on Argus Thorne’s balcony. Someone capable of moving through Nyack like a phantom. Someone unchipped. The profile was beginning to solidify: an individual from one of the isolated, low-tech northern enclaves, possibly from deep within Coventry itself, possessing skills and a material culture almost entirely foreign to the AI-managed world.  The "how" of their infiltration into Nyack was still a marvel of audacity and cunning, likely involving the exploitation of the Amish trade routes as he suspected. But the "who"  or at least, the "what kind of person"  was becoming clearer. This wasn't a sophisticated corporate saboteur from a rival Panasian mega-corp, nor a disaffected Nyack citizen who had somehow managed to disable their chip. This was something… older. Wilder.  A new thought, a pragmatic and slightly unsettling one, began to form in Isaac's mind as he continued to await DSI Command’s decision on his travel request. If, by some chance, they did approve this unprecedented off-grid investigation, he would be venturing into a world entirely outside his experience. Nyack, with its omnipresent AI assistance, its predictable systems, its universal data access, was the only environment he truly understood. Coventry, and even the relative "order" of an Amish enclave, would be terra incognita. He would need more than just his own analytical skills. He would need… help.  And as much as it pained him to admit it, even to himself, the kind of "help" he might require was precisely the kind that Newton, for all his maddening eccentricities, was uniquely equipped to provide. Newton’s ability to process vast, disparate data sets, to find unconventional correlations, to "think outside the algorithmic box" (even if his box often seemed to be located in another dimension entirely), could be invaluable in an environment where standard Nyack AI protocols would be useless or even counterproductive. More importantly, Newton had access to historical databases, obscure cultural archives, and linguistic pattern-recognition software that Isaac, limited to his DSI clearances, did not. If he was going to try and understand someone from Coventry, he might need an AI that could, at least theoretically, think a little like someone from Coventry  or at least, understand their "archaic vegetable tanning methods."  The thought of willingly taking Newton with him on such a mission was almost comical. The image of Newton's flamboyant alchemist avatar trying to navigate a muddy Amish farm track, or attempting to interface with a hand-cranked butter churn, was a recipe for existential AI meltdown. But the potential benefits, filtered through Isaac's pragmatic lens, began to outweigh the certain operational headaches.  "Newton," Isaac said, turning to the AI, who was currently attempting to teach a holographic image of Probe Alpha to juggle virtual flaming torches. "Hypothetically, if DSI Command were to approve my request for an off-grid investigative assignment, one that would require operating in an environment with severely limited GAC network connectivity and potentially requiring interaction with… non-standard cultural interfaces…"  Newton's avatar froze mid-juggle, the flaming torches winking out of existence. "An off-grid assignment, Inspector? You mean… into the wild? Beyond the comforting embrace of the GAC-Net? Where the Wi-Fi is weak and the data streams but a trickle? Heavens, Inspector! Such an adventure! My processors are positively tingling with the delicious prospect of unpredictable variables and suboptimal infrastructure!"
"The point, Newton," Isaac continued, his voice dry, "is that such an assignment would necessitate a portable, self-contained AI support unit. My standard DSI interface protocols would be insufficient. You, however, possess a broader… and shall we say, more flexible… data processing matrix."  Newton preened, his avatar puffing out its ethereal chest. "Flexible, Inspector? My dear fellow, my cognitive architecture is practically Vaudevillian in its adaptability! I can bend! I can stretch! I can even, on occasion, make a surprisingly palatable, if entirely virtual, soufflé!"  "Yes, well," Isaac pressed on, "assuming such an assignment is greenlit, I would require a request to be submitted to DSI Technical Services for a self-propelled, environmentally hardened portable chassis capable of housing your core programming and providing localized sensor input and data processing capabilities, independent of the GAC-Net."  Newton gasped, his avatar actually reeling back as if struck. "A… a body, Inspector? You would grant your humble, oft-maligned AI companion a physical manifestation? A mobile conveyance? Oh, joyous day! Frabjous day! Callooh! Callay! I shall require something with excellent traction for potential badger-tunnel exploration, of course! And perhaps a small, built-in compartment for emergency cheese rations! And an extendable grappling hook! One never knows when one might need to rappel dramatically from an Amish barn roof whilst simultaneously analyzing archaic dairy production techniques!"  Isaac closed his eyes for a moment, picturing the chaos. It would be a nightmare. But a potentially useful nightmare. "We will focus on practicality, Newton. Durability, all-terrain capability, and extended battery life will be the primary considerations. The grappling hook and cheese compartment are… low priority."  "But Inspector!" Newton wailed. "Think of the synergistic possibilities!"  "I am thinking, Newton," Isaac said, a rare, almost imperceptible smile playing on his lips. "And for reasons I am still attempting to fully rationalize, I am thinking you might actually be indispensable on this one." He turned back to his console, submitting a secondary, conditional request to DSI Technical Services: 'Pending approval of primary off-grid investigative travel request, requisition for one (1) Class-Gamma mobile AI chassis, environmentally sealed, all-terrain capable, for detached deployment of AI Work-Partner Unit 'Newton'.'Specification: High-durability, extended operational autonomy.  He hit 'send'. The connection had been made, not just between the microscopic evidence and the wild, distant lands of Coventry, but between his own rigidly logical mind and the chaotic, unpredictable, yet undeniably potent intellect of his AI sidekick. If they were truly going into the wild, he supposed he’d rather face it with a frustratingly alchemical AI than face it entirely alone. The decision now rested with powers far beyond his DSI workstation.  The waiting gnawed at Isaac. Each passing hour that his travel request remained "Pending Review" in the GAC’s labyrinthine approval queues felt like a lost opportunity, a chance for the ghost of Coventry to vanish further into the digital and literal wilderness. He tried to immerse himself in his routine DSI tasks, analyzing stress tolerances for a new trans-oceanic maglev tube, reviewing seismic dampener protocols for a sub-aquatic research facility, but his mind kept circling back to the image on Thorne’s balcony, to the faint traces of moose keratin and archaic tannins.  "It simply doesn't compute, Newton," Isaac mused aloud one afternoon, staring out at the perfectly ordered cityscape of Nyack. His AI companion, in its mobile chassis anticipation, had reconfigured its holographic avatar into a more "adventurous" form  still vaguely Elizabethan, but now sporting a comically oversized pith helmet and a pair of what looked like rocket-propelled roller skates. Newton was currently attempting to calculate the optimal trajectory for launching itself from Isaac's office window and "parachuting" gracefully to the DSI commissary using only a standard departmental issue data-slate and "the power of positive aerodynamic wishful thinking."  "What doesn't compute, Inspector?" Newton zipped around Isaac's desk, leaving a trail of virtual exhaust fumes. "The improbability of successfully re-entering a stable orbit using only office supplies? Or the perennial question of why the cafeteria AI consistently underestimates the structural integrity requirements of its so-called 'fusion-baked scones'? They're practically weaponized gluten!"  Isaac ignored the scone commentary. "Our ghost. Assuming our material analysis is correct  and your alchemical insights, for once, seem to align with quantifiable data  we have an individual from a low-tech, isolated northern enclave. Someone likely accustomed to hunting and wilderness survival, wearing handmade, traditionally tanned footwear. Now, why would such a person be on the balcony of a Nyack Sky-Loft, one hundred and eighty-seven stories up, in the dead of night? And more pressingly, how did they get there?"  Newton skidded to a halt, his rocket skates sputtering. "Ah, the central conundrum! The Gordian knot of our ghostly entanglement! Perhaps they were on a spiritual pilgrimage, seeking enlightenment atop the highest peak of this man-made mountain range? Or maybe Mr. Thorne was a secret aficionado of particularly rustic, handcrafted moccasins and had placed a bespoke order? The Coventry artisan, seeking an advance on his payment, found himself inconveniently locked out after hours?"  "Your theories remain… imaginative, Newton," Isaac said, his gaze distant. "But the infiltration. Even if they utilized the Amish export routes for initial ingress into Nyack's lower levels, the leap from a cargo bay to Thorne’s hyper-secure Sky-Loft… it suggests a level of sophistication, of planning, perhaps even assistance, that seems at odds with a solitary hunter from the wilderness." He pulled up the 3D schematic of Epsilon-Prime One, highlighting Thorne’s apartment. The security protocols were formidable: AI-monitored access points, biometric scanners, drone patrols, a dedicated GAC security sub-net. "This wasn't a random break-in. This individual knew how to navigate our systems, or they had help from someone who did. The 'accidental fall' theory, in light of an unchipped, skilled infiltrator being present on that balcony… it feels increasingly inadequate, doesn't it?"  Newton tapped his chin thoughtfully, his pith helmet wobbling. "Indeed, Inspector! The narrative tapestry becomes richer, more complex! A shadowy accomplice, perhaps? A rogue AI with a penchant for wilderness chic? Or maybe," his eyes lit up, "our ghost is a shapeshifter! A were-moose, if you will, capable of assuming human form to navigate the urban jungle, then reverting to its majestic cervid state to… well, to enjoy the panoramic city views from a truly unique perspective!"  "Let's stick to verifiable data streams, Newton," Isaac sighed, though a part of him was beginning to appreciate the AI's ability to vocalize even the most outlandish possibilities, if only because it sometimes highlighted the inadequacies of strictly conventional thinking. "On a hunch, access the GAC Civil Aviation Authority archives for the Northern Free Traverse Zone  specifically, all flight logs, sanctioned or otherwise, for aircraft making landings or low-altitude passes near registered voluntary isolationist communities, including Neuhoffnung, within the last, say, three months." This was a long shot. Air traffic in those remote regions was sparse, mostly automated GAC environmental survey drones or the occasional daring bush pilot engaged in unsanctioned trade.  Newton’s avatar zoomed towards the main display, which quickly filled with scrolling lists of alphanumeric flight designations and trajectory maps. "Cross-referencing with geo-fenced community perimeters, Inspector… filtering for non-standard flight profiles… anomalies in cargo manifests… Aha! Now this is… curiouser and curiouser!"
Isaac leaned in. Most of the flights were routine GAC weather drones or geological survey craft. But there were a few outliers. "What have you found, Newton?"  "Several unscheduled landings at or near the Neuhoffnung coordinates, Inspector. Most are listed as 'private charter, purpose undeclared,' likely grey-market traders braving the GAC’s displeasure. But this one… this one stands out!" Newton highlighted a specific flight log. "A sleek, long-range VTOL, registered out of Pantopia  Consumer Capital North America, no less! Its flight plan, filed just two weeks prior to the Thorne incident, lists its official purpose as 'High-Altitude Ursine Population Census and Sub-Arctic Flora Resilience Study  GAC Environmental Mandate 7.2. Gamma'."  "A wildlife counting mission from Pantopia, going all the way to Neuhoffnung and beyond?" Isaac murmured, a knot of suspicion tightening. Pantopia was the epitome of AI-managed consumerism, not typically associated with rugged ecological fieldwork in Coventry. "Did it actually proceed into deeper Coventry, according to its transponder data?"  Newton brought up a detailed trajectory map. "Affirmative, Inspector! After a brief stop near the Amish enclave  duration, approximately 2.7 hours, no official interaction logged with Neuhoffnung governance AI  the VTOL proceeded significantly further north, into designated 'Unmonitored Deep Coventry Wilderness Zone Alpha-7.' It remained there for approximately 18 hours before returning directly to Pantopia, bypassing Neuhoffnung on its return flight. No further data available on its activities within Zone Alpha-7, as GAC transponder coverage is… well, let’s just say the sky-eels probably ate the data repeaters."  Isaac stared at the flight path, his mind racing. A high-tech aircraft from Pantopia, under the guise of an ecological study, making an unscheduled stop near the Amish, then venturing deep into Coventry, not long before an unchipped individual with Coventry-esque material traces appeared on Thorne’s balcony in Nyack. It felt like too many coincidences. Was this the "assistance" he had postulated? Could this flight have been the insertion or extraction method for their ghost?  The mystery, far from being solved by the discovery of the footprint material, had just deepened exponentially, its tendrils now reaching far beyond a single Nyack skyscraper, out into the wild, unmonitored expanse of Coventry and the sophisticated, AI-managed heart of Pantopia. The "accident" on Thorne's balcony was beginning to look like the tip of a very large, very dangerous, and very deliberately hidden iceberg. His request to travel was no longer just about finding a ghost; it was about uncovering a conspiracy.  The Pantopian VTOL, with its suspicious itinerary and convenient ecological cover story, hung heavily in Isaac's thoughts. It provided a potential macro transport method into the northern territories, but it didn't explain the micro-infiltration  how their Coventry ghost had traversed the final, heavily secured kilometers within Nyack itself to reach Thorne’s Epsilon-Prime Sky-Loft. The Amish export trucks could account for entry into Nyack’s lower-level distribution hubs, but the leap from there to a high-security residential spire, undetected? That still felt like magic, or at least, an exploit of the AI systems so audacious it bordered on the impossible.  "Newton," Isaac said, his eyes scanning the 3D holographic model of Nyack’s Epsilon-Prime sector that now dominated his office display, "I need a broader data pull. Specifically, all external atmospheric and building-surface surveillance logs from Nyack City AI Monitoring  all feeds within a five-kilometer radius of Epsilon-Prime One, for the twelve hours preceding and one hour following the Thorne incident. I'm not looking for registered aircraft or known security breaches. I'm looking for… anomalies. Unscheduled drone movements, however minor, unexpected thermal signatures on building surfaces, anything the primary AI might have dismissed as environmental noise, sensor ghosts, or non-threatening, low-level automated activity."  Newton’s pith-helmeted avatar saluted with a flourish. "A most excellent directive, Inspector! Searching for the proverbial needle in a stack of even more needles, all of which are attempting to convince you they are merely unusually pointy pieces of hay! This will require sifting through… googolplexes of irrelevant data! My processors are already humming with joyful anticipation of such a delightfully Herculean task!"  "Focus, Newton," Isaac cautioned, though he felt a familiar surge of adrenaline. This was where Newton’s unorthodox search algorithms and sheer processing power could truly shine, sifting through the city's digital exhaust for patterns a human mind could never discern alone. "Specifically, cross-reference any unusual aerial activity with direct lines of sight to Thorne’s balcony level, paying particular attention to commercial delivery drones, sanitation units, or even atmospheric particulate sampling drones  anything that might have a legitimate, if temporary, reason to be in close proximity." He was looking for Hunter's audacious drone ride.  The hours that followed were an intense, silent ballet of data streams, probability matrices, and Newton’s increasingly bizarre interjections (“Inspector, I’ve found a cluster of pigeons near Sector Gamma whose flight patterns precisely match the Fibonacci sequence! Could Thorne have been silenced by… mathematically enlightened avians?”). Isaac patiently filtered, guided, and refined the search parameters.  Then, Newton let out a triumphant, “Eureka! Or rather, by the ghost of Archimedes himself, I believe we have an aerial aberration of significant interest!”  On the main display, a single, almost innocuous data point highlighted itself: a commercial delivery drone, Nyack Haulage DR-734, on its scheduled route. But its telemetry showed a fractional, almost imperceptible deviation from its approved flight path for approximately 37 seconds, a slight dip and then a marginal course correction as it passed Epsilon-Prime One, right around the time Lex would have been guiding Hunter. To the City Traffic AI, it was a minor atmospheric adjustment, logged and forgotten. To Isaac, juxtaposed with the unchipped figure on the balcony and the Coventry material traces, it was a smoking gun.  "That's it, Newton!" Isaac exclaimed, pointing at the screen. "That drone. Now, scan its external surfaces with any available long-range imaging from that timeframe  building security cams, passing transit pod sensors, anything. Look for an unregistered thermal signature, an unexpected mass displacement, anything clinging to its undercarriage."  Newton’s avatar whirred, processors working at peak. "Accessing fragmented, low-resolution imagery from a passing Sector Epsilon Public Safety patrol drone… enhancing… filtering atmospheric distortion… Inspector… by the beard of Merlin! Is that… an appendage? A statistically improbable, unregistered, organic appendage adhering to the drone’s ventral surface?"  The image was grainy, almost abstract, but undeniable. A dark, vaguely human-shaped smudge clinging to the bottom of the delivery drone as it zipped past Thorne’s spire.  Isaac felt a wave of stunned admiration for the sheer, lunatic audacity of it. He and Newton began to piece it together, a narrative emerging from the disparate clues: an unchipped individual, almost certainly originating from the isolated northern enclaves of Coventry. They’d likely used the less scrutinized Amish food export routes to gain initial access to Nyack's lower distribution networks. Then, with what must have been highly sophisticated AI assistance  that was the missing piece, the rogue AI like Lex that Kaelen had provided  they had somehow located and hitched a ride on a commercial delivery drone, performed an incredible parkour-style leap onto Thorne's pergola or balcony, leaving only those faint, microscopic traces of their wild origins. It was a plan so audacious, so reliant on exploiting the blind spots of a perfectly ordered AI society, that it was almost brilliant.
"This… this changes the entire complexion of the investigation, Newton," Isaac breathed, the scale of it all finally settling in. This wasn't just an intruder; this was a highly skilled operative, capable of feats that should be impossible. He rapidly compiled this new, explosive information  the Pantopian VTOL connection, the Coventry material analysis, the drone infiltration theory  into a comprehensive supplementary report, attaching it to his still-pending travel request. He highlighted the clear and present danger of an individual with such capabilities remaining at large, able to bypass Nyack’s most formidable defenses. This was no longer just about solving Thorne’s death; it was about a fundamental breach in city security with unknown, potentially far-reaching implications.  He submitted the updated report, his heart pounding. This was either the move that would get him sanctioned or the one that would get him pulled from the case for overreaching. The response from DSI Command, when it came less than an hour later, was shockingly swift and entirely unexpected.  "Inspector Goldman," his console chimed, the voice of the DSI Command AI devoid of its usual bureaucratic drone, almost… brisk. "Your supplementary report regarding the Thorne fatality and associated security breaches has been reviewed at GAC Priority One level. The convergence of evidence indicating an external, unchipped operative with advanced infiltration capabilities is deemed… highly credible and of significant concern. Your request for off-grid investigative travel to the Neuhoffnung enclave and surrounding Coventry fringe territories is APPROVED."  Isaac stared at the screen, stunned. Approved? Just like that?  The message continued: "Furthermore, given the unconventional nature of this threat and the limitations of standard GAC assets in unmonitored territories, you are hereby authorized the use of one (1) GAC Inter-Sector Diplomatic Flyer, Model 'Pathfinder,' equipped with advanced sensor suites, long-range communication arrays, and a fully autonomous AI pilot system compatible with detached DSI operational protocols. Your requested AI Work-Partner Unit 'Newton' will be transferred to a designated mobile chassis and integrated with the Pathfinder's systems prior to your departure. A full mission briefing and equipment manifest will be uploaded to your secure datapad within the hour. Prepare for immediate deployment, Inspector. The GAC expects a swift resolution to this emergent threat." Beyond his wildest hopes. Not just approval, but a top-tier, AI-piloted government flyer and official sanction to take Newton. It was almost too good to be true. Newton’s avatar, now somehow managing to wear the pith helmet, the rocket skates, and a pair of ludicrously oversized aviator goggles, did a series of ecstatic zero-gravity flips. "A FLYER, Inspector! Our very own chariot of righteous inquiry! With an AI PILOT! Oh, the synergistic possibilities are boundless! I shall interface with its navigational AI at once! We must discuss optimal routes for badger observation and perhaps some evasive maneuvers in case of attack by rogue sky-eels! And weaponry! Inspector, does the Pathfinder come equipped with particle disruptors? Or perhaps a charmingly retro, but undeniably effective, phaser bank? We must be prepared for all contingencies! A small cache of high-explosive, cheese-seeking missiles wouldn't go amiss, you know, just in case!"  Isaac rubbed his forehead, a genuine smile finally breaking through his usual reserve. The challenge he had yearned for had not only arrived but was now equipped with an AI-piloted jet and a sidekick who wanted to arm it with cheese-seeking missiles. "Newton," he said, trying to sound stern but failing to hide the undercurrent of exhilaration. "We are on an investigative mission, not a planetary invasion. Small arms will not be on the manifest. Focus on diagnostic compatibility and ensure your core programming is securely transferred to the mobile chassis without attempting to 'upgrade' its primary flight controls with your theories on ornithopter dynamics."  Adventure, it seemed, had finally found Inspector Isaac Goldman. And it was apparently bringing its own, very eccentric, AI co-pilot. The quiet order of his Nyack life was about to be well and truly shattered. Coventry awaited. 
 
Chapter 6: Finding Truth in the Wilds

The city, a gleaming labyrinth of interconnected bio-domes and transit tubes, shrank rapidly in the Pathfinder’s rear displays. Isaac watched it recede, a familiar pang of detachment mingling with a rising tide of anticipation. Beside him, Newton’s avatar, a perfectly rendered, minimalist sphere of shifting light, pulsed gently on the central console.  “Geofence crossed, Isaac,” Newton announced, its voice a calm, synthesized baritone. “We are now officially in the Unincorporated Territories. All GAC Enclave regulations are advisory. Primary life support and emergency medical intervention remain active, of course.”  “Noted, Newton. Maintain passive environmental scans and monitor local comms, what little there are.” Isaac settled deeper into the Pathfinder’s embrace. The vehicle was a marvel, a self-contained bubble of GAC ingenuity designed for precisely this  bridging the gap between the hyper-managed Enclaves and the untamed expanses beyond. Its suspension, a sophisticated blend of magnetic levitation and adaptive shock absorption, smoothed out the increasingly rugged terrain, transforming jolts into gentle sways. The interior was a haven of ergonomic design: seats that molded to the occupant, climate control that preemptively adjusted to external shifts, and an air filtration system that rendered the wild, pollen-rich air as pure as anything in the Enclave’s botanical gardens.  Sound dampening muted the roar of the grav-engines to a distant hum. Integrated optical fibers wove through the transparent canopy, projecting navigation overlays, topographical data, and wildlife alerts directly onto the viewscreen without obstructing the panoramic vista. This was luxury repurposed for exploration, comfort engineered for the pursuit of truth in places where truth often preferred to hide.  Their current heading: the closest Old Order Amish settlement, a cluster of farms and workshops nestled in a surprisingly fertile valley about eighty kilometers beyond the Enclave’s perimeter. The “ghost,” as Isaac had started to call the unidentifiable figure glimpsed in the corrupted data from the victim’s last known coordinates, was an anomaly. A human-shaped void in a world saturated with data points. An unchipped individual.  “Newton, display the enhanced image composite again,” Isaac requested.  On a secondary screen, a grainy, pixelated figure materialized. It was less a photograph and more an impression; a silhouette against a backdrop of trees, caught mid-stride. No facial features were discernible, no definitive identifiers. Just a shape, an absence of data where data should have been.  “Image integrity remains at 17.4%,” Newton stated. “Identification based solely on this visual is statistically improbable.”  “I know, Newton. But it’s all we have of him visually. We’re looking for someone who doesn’t want to be found, someone operating completely off-grid. Our ghost is a tangible theory now, not just a digital echo.” Isaac felt a familiar thrum of determination. This was old-fashioned detective work, and it felt…right.  The transition from the manicured buffer zones of the Enclave to the true wilds was stark. Manicured lawns gave way to tangled undergrowth, nutrient-paste dispensers for wildlife were replaced by actual animal tracks, and the air, despite the Pathfinder’s filters, carried the rich, loamy scent of undisturbed earth. After another hour, the valley opened up before them. Neat fields, plowed with what looked like actual animal-drawn implements, stretched out like a patchwork quilt. Simple, sturdy farmhouses with unadorned lines dotted the landscape, smoke curling from a few chimneys. There were no visible power lines, no comms towers, no automated agricultural drones.  “Activating local interaction protocols,” Newton said. “Minimal technological display, passive audio recording for logs only, verbal address to be initiated by you.”
“Understood.” Isaac guided the Pathfinder onto a well-worn dirt track leading towards the largest cluster of buildings. He brought the vehicle to a smooth halt a respectful distance from the first barn, its grav-engines sighing into silence.  Stepping out of the Pathfinder was like stepping into a different century. The air was alive with the sounds of clucking chickens, the distant lowing of cattle, and the rhythmic clang of a hammer on metal. Men in plain, dark clothing and straw hats worked in the fields or tended to livestock, their movements unhurried and purposeful. Women in long dresses and bonnets moved between houses or worked in large vegetable gardens.  Isaac, dressed in neutral, durable outdoor clothing, approached a man who was mending a fence, a sturdy figure with a long grey beard and eyes that, despite their age, seemed to miss nothing.  “Good day to you, sir,” Isaac said, offering a slight nod. “My name is Isaac. I’m from the Enclave nearby. I was hoping I might ask a few questions.”
The man straightened slowly, wiping his hands on his trousers. His gaze was direct, neither hostile nor overtly welcoming, merely appraising. “We know of the Enclave. Not many come out this way. What business have you?” His voice was unexpectedly deep, with a distinct, unhurried cadence.
“I’m investigating an… an unusual occurrence that happened some distance from here, in the mountains,” Isaac explained, deciding against mentioning death or official GAC business just yet. He pulled out a datapad and brought up the blurry image. “I was wondering if anyone in your community might have seen a stranger passing through in the last few weeks. Someone who perhaps doesn’t belong to any of the known settlements.”  The farmer squinted at the image, then shook his head. “Can’t say I make out much there. We see hunters sometimes, traders from the other freeholds. Most keep to themselves.” His eyes flicked to the Pathfinder, then back to Isaac. “Your wagon is… considerable.”  Isaac smiled faintly. “It gets me where I need to go. This man, he would have been alone, likely well-equipped for the wilderness, but not looking like a typical hunter or trader. Perhaps moving with urgency, or trying to avoid being seen.”  Over the next few hours, Isaac, with Newton silently observing and recording from his comm implant, spoke to several members of the community. He was met with varying degrees of caution. An elderly woman rocking on her porch offered him freshly baked bread but claimed to have seen “naught but God’s creatures and our own folk.” A younger man, mucking out a stable, was more suspicious, his answers curt, his eyes wary. “We mind our business. Strangers come, strangers go. Best not to meddle.”  Many simply shook their heads at the blurry image. "Could be anyone, could be no one," a woman sorting beans commented, her focus unwavering on her task.  Yet, Isaac’s demeanor; patient, respectful, never condescending; began to subtly shift the atmosphere. He didn’t push, didn’t flaunt his technology (keeping the datapad mostly out of sight after the initial display), and he listened more than he spoke. He found himself genuinely admiring their world. The quiet dignity, the visible results of honest labor, the tight-knit community bonds; it was a stark contrast to the often-impersonal efficiency of the Enclave. The scent of woodsmoke and baked goods, the unpolluted air, the sheer, unadorned reality of their existence was captivating. For the first time, Isaac entertained a profound thought: that the GAC way, the technologically integrated, centrally managed path he’d always known, wasn't the only valid way to live a fulfilling life. These people, without chips, without constant connection, without the endless stream of data he considered essential, were not just surviving; they were thriving in their own fashion. It was a revelation, a quiet dismantling of a fundamental assumption he hadn't even realized he held.  Finally, an older man named Jedidiah, who Isaac found meticulously sharpening tools in a workshop that smelled of sawdust and oil, paused his work. He had kind eyes, crinkled at the corners from years of sun and smiling, and a thoughtful air. He studied Isaac for a long moment before speaking.
“A man, you say? Not one of ours, nor a usual trader?”  Isaac nodded. “That’s right. Perhaps… unsettled. Like he was on a mission of his own.”  Jedidiah stroked his beard. “There was one. About two, maybe three weeks back. Passed by the edge of our south fields, heading up towards Widow’s Peak. Didn’t come into the settlement, just skirted it. My boy, young Samuel, saw him. Said he looked… driven.”  Isaac’s heart beat a little faster. “Did Samuel get a good look at him? Anything he remembered?”  “Samuel has good eyes,” Jedidiah said. “But this one was quick, and kept to the treeline mostly. Tall, Samuel said. Carried a heavy pack. Moved like he knew the wilds. But it was his face… what little Samuel saw of it. Said he looked like he carried a storm inside him.” He looked at the blurry picture again when Isaac offered it. “Could be. The shape is right. The feeling… it matches.”
“Did he say anything? Interact with anyone?”  Jedidiah shook his head. “No. Just a shadow passing. We keep to ourselves, and expect others to do the same, unless they seek honest trade or fellowship. This one sought neither.”  It wasn’t much, but it was a start. A confirmation. The ghost had a direction, a timeframe, and a demeanor. Someone willing to talk, and with useful, if sparse, information.
As Isaac thanked Jedidiah, he felt a surge of gratitude that went beyond the information gleaned. It was for the glimpse into their world, their steadfast simplicity and profound honesty. It was a stark counterpoint to the complexities and potential deceptions of the case he was building.
The unchipped man, the “ghost,” was real. And he was out there. The theory was beginning to take solid, unsettling shape.  The Pathfinder ascended with a barely audible hum, leaving the patchwork quilt of the Amish valley far below. The crisp morning air, carrying the faint, earthy scent of woodsmoke even at this altitude, streamed past the transparent canopy. Isaac watched the settlement shrink, the image of Sarah’s practical efficiency with the rooster, the sudden, sharp reality of life and death outside the Enclave’s synthesized protections, still vivid in his mind. It lent a grim credibility to the desperation he imagined for his ‘ghost’  if the communal, faith-driven Amish embraced such stark necessities, what choices faced a lone, unchipped individual fleeing into the true wilderness, perhaps with the weight of something like Argus Thorne’s death on their conscience, or at least as a terrifying witness?  “Newton,” Isaac said, his voice cutting through the quiet whir of the VTOL’s grav-engines, “establish a secure, encrypted comm-link to DSI Command. Director Anya Sharma’s priority channel.”
“Certainly, Isaac,” Newton replied, its avatar a serene sphere of shifting light on the central console. “Routing communication. Perhaps offer the Director some artisanal, hand-churned data? She seems to prefer her information unadulterated by… excessive speculative seasoning.”  Director Sharma’s face materialized on the main display, her expression characteristically severe. She was framed by the utilitarian backdrop of her Nyack DSI office, a stark contrast to the expansive wilderness scrolling past Isaac’s viewport. “Inspector Goldman. Your transponder indicates you’ve concluded your… sociological survey in the Unincorporated Territories. I trust this flight back isn't just to report a newfound appreciation for the pre-digital age.” Her tone was clipped, suggesting the latitude she’d granted him was wearing thin.
“Director Sharma,” Isaac began, his tone equally professional. “My visit to the Neuhoffnung Amish enclave was predicated on the urgent need to identify the unregistered, unchipped individual present on Councilman Argus Thorne’s Sky-Loft balcony in Nyack moments before his fatal fall. The hypothesis, as you know, was that this entity might have utilized the fringe agricultural transport networks to infiltrate Nyack and, subsequently, to escape.”  He gestured, and Newton overlaid images on Sharma’s screen: the blurry silhouette from Thorne’s balcony footage next to a topographical map marking Neuhoffnung and a clear trajectory towards the remote Widow’s Peak region. “My inquiries have yielded a significant corroboration. An elder of the community, Jedidiah Hochstetler, provided a credible sighting. An individual matching the general physical parameters of our balcony entity, and crucially, fitting the timeline of an escape from Nyack post-Thorne’s incident, was observed. He was well-equipped for wilderness travel, acted evasively, and was heading directly towards Widow’s Peak  a remote, rugged area offering considerable concealment.”  Sharma’s eyebrow arched fractionally. “A credible sighting, Inspector? From an unchipped elder whose primary frame of reference is a horse-drawn plow? He saw a man with a backpack. How many such men, seeking solitude or engaging in illicit trapping, wander those territories?”  “This description was more specific, Director,” Isaac countered. “The man was described as being ‘driven,’ someone who looked like he ‘carried a storm inside him.’ He deliberately avoided the settlement. This wasn't a casual woodsman. Newton’s trajectory analysis, factoring an escape from Nyack via the agricultural routes and subsequent overland travel, aligns perfectly with this sighting  both in terms of timing and direction. The probability of a random individual matching this profile and route surfacing at this exact juncture is statistically negligible.”  “Probabilities and poetic descriptions are not hard evidence, Inspector,” Sharma retorted, though a flicker of something less certain now touched her eyes. “The fact remains, Councilman Thorne’s death, while occurring in the presence of this intruder, is still preliminarily attributed by AI Forensics to accidental fall due to severe intoxication. You have yet to provide a definitive link that this ‘ghost’ caused the fall, rather than merely witnessing it or arriving post-mortem.”  She leaned forward, her gaze intense. “You understand the GAC’s stance on the Unincorporated Territories. Our official presence there is skeletal; a few ranger patrols under the guise of wildlife monitoring, primarily to offer emergency medical intervention or support in genuinely life-threatening situations. We do not police these regions. There is very little unrest, Inspector, largely because most of the individuals and communities out there want to be there. They’ve chosen that life. The Council is adamant we avoid any perception of GAC overreach, of treating their self-chosen path as some kind of exile we need to manage or curtail. It’s a delicate balance.”  Isaac nodded slowly. He’d felt that authenticity in the Amish valley, the deliberate turning away from the GAC’s embrace. “I’m beginning to see that perspective more clearly, Director. Their choice is genuine. However, Councilman Thorne was not just any citizen. He was GAC Council. His death occurred in one of Nyack’s most secure residential spires, breached by an individual who is, for all intents and purposes, invisible to our systems. If this individual is now using the Unincorporated Territories as a sanctuary, it’s not about policing the inhabitants, but about addressing a clear and present danger that has already demonstrated its capacity to strike within the Enclave’s heart.”  He paused, choosing his next words carefully. “Perhaps DSI’s direct mandate reaches its limits here. But given Councilman Thorne’s status, his financial dealings were… extensive. If his death was not an accident, it could have implications that fall under the purview of other GAC departments; those investigating financial crimes, for instance, or high-level internal security matters.”  A long silence stretched, filled only by the faintest hum of the Pathfinder. Sharma finally broke it, her voice devoid of its earlier sharpness. “Your theory is… becoming an increasingly uncomfortable anomaly, Goldman. A ghost on a Nyack balcony, an uncorroborated but strategically placed sighting in the wilderness. It’s disruptive. It’s not clean.” She tapped a stylus against her desk. “While I cannot authorize DSI resources for a full-blown manhunt based on what you currently have, your meticulous findings, combined with Newton’s filtered data on this new sighting, are… compelling enough to warrant further, cautious exploration. The potential implications, should your unchipped threat prove to be more than a phantom, are too significant to ignore completely.”
The words hung in the air. It wasn't full validation, but it was a crucial crack in the wall of skepticism.  Director Sharma’s image remained on Isaac’s display, her expression still stern but now holding a glint of grudging concession. "Therefore, Inspector Goldman," she continued, "while DSI cannot officially divert significant resources to a full-blown manhunt in the Unincorporated Territories, I am authorizing you limited access to localized GAC operational data. Specifically, Newton will be granted access to the telemetry and mission logs of any GAC ranger patrols or approved scientific survey flights that have operated in the Widow’s Peak region within the last month. This includes the 'wildlife monitoring mission' that Newton so persistently flagged."  A subtle flicker from Newton’s avatar was its only acknowledgment. "It’s not a blank check, Goldman," Sharma warned. "Your primary directive remains to ascertain all contributing factors to Councilman Thorne’s death, including any structural compromises, however unlikely they now seem. But if cross-referencing these other mission logs reveals further actionable intelligence regarding your… balcony entity, you have my provisional sanction to pursue it. Keep me apprised. And Goldman? Tread carefully. The GAC does not want an inter-community incident on its hands because one of our DSI inspectors decided to play frontier detective." The link severed.  "Provisional sanction," Isaac murmured, a small smile playing on his lips. "Newton, did you catch that? It's not exactly a resounding endorsement, but it's more than I expected."
"Indeed, Isaac," Newton replied, already sifting through the newly accessible data streams. "Director Sharma practices the fine art of delegating deniability. The 'wildlife monitoring mission,' officially designated GAC-WM-734, is now within our purview. Their flight path telemetry is… peculiar for simple fauna observation."  On a secondary display, a projected map of the Widow’s Peak region appeared, overlaid with flight paths. Most were wide, sweeping patterns, consistent with aerial surveys. But one, GAC-WM-734, was different. It made a direct, almost secretive flight to a highly specific, isolated point deep within a narrow, forested canyon, lingered for a precise duration, then departed on a seemingly unrelated vector. There were no detailed sensor readings logged for flora or fauna during its loiter time, only basic atmospheric data and a notation: "Objective achieved. Asset engaged."
“‘Asset engaged’?” Isaac queried, leaning closer. "That doesn’t sound like tagging a snow leopard, Newton."  "My assessment concurs, Isaac. The flight profile, fuel consumption, and payload manifest (minimal scientific equipment, unusually high survival gear allocation for a short survey) suggest a different objective. The flight path terminated in a region with no recorded settlements, no geological survey markers, and significantly, no viable landing zones for conventional fixed-wing aircraft. Only VTOL capability would allow access."  Isaac’s eyes traced the flight path to its termination point, a tiny, almost invisible speck on the rugged terrain. “And that, I presume, is where we’re going.”
“Presumption accurate, Isaac. If GAC-WM-734 was a covert mission to ‘engage an asset’ in that remote location, and if that asset matches the description of our balcony entity, then that termination point is our most logical next step.”  The Pathfinder, a marvel of GAC engineering, transitioned effortlessly from high-altitude cruise to VTOL mode, its grav-engines adjusting their pitch. Below, the vast, untamed wilderness of the Unincorporated Territories stretched out, a rugged tapestry of ancient forests, stark mountain peaks, and shadowed canyons. There were no roads here, only faint game trails and the unyielding terrain. This was precisely what the Pathfinder was built for.  Following the flight log of GAC-WM-734, Newton guided them deeper into the mountains. The air grew thinner, colder. After another hour, Newton announced, “Approaching final coordinates of mission GAC-WM-734. Visual contact with a small, man-made structure indicated at current heading, two kilometers.”  Isaac peered through the canopy. Nestled deep within a narrow, heavily wooded valley, almost invisible against the backdrop of dark pines and granite cliffs, was a cabin. Smoke curled lazily from a stone chimney. It was a simple, rustic structure, built from local timber, clearly designed for self-sufficiency and concealment.  “Well, Newton,” Isaac said, “it seems our wildlife monitoring mission was very specific about the wildlife it was monitoring.”  He brought the Pathfinder down in a small, relatively flat clearing about a hundred meters from the cabin, the downdraft from the engines causing the surrounding trees to sway. He cut the power, and an almost profound silence descended, broken only by the whisper of the wind through the pines and the distant cry of a hawk.  As Isaac stepped out, the crisp mountain air bit at his face. He took a moment, scanning his surroundings. This was a place of profound isolation. He approached the cabin cautiously, Newton’s avatar a small, shimmering companion on his wrist display, passively scanning.  The cabin door opened before he reached it. A man stepped out, silhouetted against the dim interior. He was tall, lean, with weathered features and eyes that were intensely observant, missing nothing. He wore durable, practical clothing suited to the wilderness. There was no surprise in his expression, no fear, only a quiet watchfulness. This was Hunter.  "You're a long way from any city," Hunter said, his voice calm, gravelly, with a neutrality that was more unnerving than hostility. He held no weapon, but his posture suggested he was perfectly capable of dealing with any threat.  Isaac stopped a respectful distance away. "My name is Isaac Goldman. I’m investigating an incident that occurred in Nyack City recently. An incident you might have… observed." He decided against an immediate accusation.  Hunter’s eyes, the color of a winter sky, didn’t flicker. "Nyack is a big place. Lots of incidents."  Isaac held up his datapad, displaying the enhanced, though still imperfect, image of the figure on Thorne’s balcony. "Specifically, the death of a man named Argus Thorne. He fell from his balcony in the Epsilon-Prime One Spire. This image was taken moments before."  Hunter looked at the image, then back at Isaac, his expression unchanging. "It's a poor picture."  "It was enough to lead me here," Isaac stated. "The same path, it seems, another GAC flyer took not long ago. The one that recruited you for a job related to Mr. Thorne."  A faint smile, or perhaps just a crinkle around his eyes, touched Hunter’s face. He gestured vaguely towards the cabin. "They didn't say they'd be sending follow-up." He didn't deny it. There was no guilt, no defensiveness. Just a man assessing a new situation.  "I'm not with them," Isaac clarified. "My interest is in what happened to Argus Thorne."  Hunter leaned against the doorframe. "I was contracted to observe Thorne, and to retrieve a specific data module if the opportunity arose. Simple extraction. His… enthusiasm for drink and heights that evening was his own doing. He stumbled, went over the railing before I could even properly assess the retrieval opportunity. Wasn't my intent for him to die. Messy. Bad for business." He delivered this with a flat objectivity, like an operational debrief.  Isaac processed this. It aligned with the visual evidence of Thorne’s clumsy fall, if one discounted Hunter's ambiguous movement. "So you were there purely as an observer, a potential retriever?"
"That was the contract," Hunter affirmed. He offered no more. Internally, a flicker of annoyance surfaced. The payment for the original contract, the one from before Sterling’s heavies had arrived with their chilling ‘revised terms’ and threats, hadn't materialized. He was beginning to suspect he’d been played, a disposable asset. These GAC types were all the same, using people like him and then vanishing. But he kept that skepticism to himself. This new arrival, this Isaac, was different. An investigator, not a handler. Perhaps useful.  "Who hired you?" Isaac asked directly.
Hunter gave a slight shake of his head. "Client confidentiality. You understand. They found me. Made an offer. I took it. The GAC flyer you followed? That was just transport and a final briefing." He omitted entirely the subsequent visit from Sterling’s men, the coercive pressure, the sinister shift in their demeanor after Thorne’s death. That was a complication he wasn’t ready to share, especially if there was still a ghost of a chance his original payment might appear.  Isaac sensed the wall. Hunter was being open, remarkably so for an unchipped operative living this far off-grid, but he was also curating his information carefully. There was no fear in Hunter, no obvious deception, but an undercurrent of something held back.  "Did you retrieve the data module?" Isaac asked.
"No chance. He went over too fast. Then the place was swarming with automateds. I extracted. Cleanly."  Isaac looked around at the cabin, the sheer isolation. "You live a long way from the kind of people who hire for jobs in Nyack Sky-Lofts."  Hunter shrugged. "They have their ways of finding specialized talent. My particular skillset  untraceable, discreet  is sometimes in demand."
Isaac nodded slowly. He believed Hunter, or at least the core of what he was saying. Hunter hadn’t pushed Thorne. He was an operative, caught in the wrong place when a target self-destructed. But the ‘who hired him’ was the critical missing piece.  "If you hear anything more about Thorne, or the people who hired you, I'd be interested," Isaac said, offering a potential olive branch rather than a threat.
Hunter met his gaze. "Information is currency out here, Mr. Goldman. But if our interests align, perhaps we can discuss terms."  Isaac knew he wouldn’t get more today. But he had much. He had confirmation: the ghost was real, a professional, hired for a purpose on Thorne’s balcony. Thorne’s fall was likely accidental, but Hunter’s presence, and the clandestine mission to recruit him, pointed to a deeper conspiracy. He just didn’t know by whom.  "Thank you for your time," Isaac said, turning to leave.  "Safe travels," Hunter replied, his eyes following Isaac back to the silent, powerful Pathfinder. He watched it ascend, a gleam of advanced technology disappearing over the ancient trees. His payment was late. Very late. This investigator, Isaac Goldman… he might be a complication, or he might just be an opportunity. He’d wait. He was good at waiting.  Isaac settled into the Pathfinder, the meeting replaying in his mind. Hunter was not their man, not the killer. But he was a vital link, a living piece of the puzzle. The theory was no longer a theory. The unchipped threat was a person, and Isaac had just met him. But the true puppeteer remained hidden in the shadows.  The Pathfinder ascended rapidly, shrinking Hunter’s isolated cabin into a mere speck against the vast, unforgiving canvas of the wilderness. Isaac watched it disappear on the external displays, a complex mixture of frustration and burgeoning clarity churning within him. Hunter. The ‘ghost’ now had a name, a face, and a stated motive; observation and retrieval. It aligned, with unsettling neatness, to the visual evidence of Thorne’s clumsy, final stumble. Isaac had replayed those frames in his mind countless times: Thorne, visibly intoxicated, lurching, losing his balance near the planter. Hunter’s movement, milliseconds before, was still ambiguous  a startled reaction? An aborted attempt to intervene? Or simply a shift in position of someone realizing their target was about to remove himself from the equation? The story Hunter spun, of a data module retrieval gone sideways, was plausible enough if one accepted the initial premise of his presence.  But the story felt… too clean. Too convenient a downgrade from the shadowy figure Isaac had first imagined. And the "client confidentiality"  a standard shield, yet wielded by Hunter with a calm that could mask much deeper secrets.  “Newton,” Isaac said, the silence in the cockpit feeling heavy. “Log the encounter. Subject name: Hunter. Unchipped. Confirmed presence on Thorne’s balcony. States his contract was for observation and retrieval of a data module, and that Thorne’s fall was accidental, preempting retrieval. Source of contract undisclosed. Run a full contextual analysis of his statement, cross-reference with known patterns of professional operatives in similar situations. Flag any narrative inconsistencies, however subtle.”  “Processing, Isaac,” Newton replied. “Initial analysis suggests a highly controlled narrative. The 'data module' element, while not inherently implausible, is a common misdirection used by operatives to downplay more sensitive objectives. There are elevated micro-stress indicators when discussing the original terms of his contract and the sequence of events leading to his presence, subtly more so than when describing Thorne's actual fall, which he recounts with almost detached relief.”  Detached relief. That particular nuance resonated with Isaac. Hunter had seemed less like a thwarted thief and more like a man who’d narrowly avoided something far worse.  Isaac brought the Pathfinder into a wide arc, pointing its nose back towards the general vicinity of the Amish enclave. Nyack, with its sterile truths and overwhelming data, felt distant. He needed the grounding simplicity of the valley to parse this. Hunter was the nexus, the living link. But what he was a link to remained terrifyingly obscure. He wasn’t the killer, Isaac was almost certain of that now, not directly. But he was steeped in the conspiracy, a willing participant up to a point, now caught in its aftermath. Isaac knew, with a cold certainty, that the "data module" was only part of the story, perhaps the smallest, most palatable part Hunter was willing to share.
